<p>After the success at Orl√©ans, Joan insisted that the Armagnac forces should advance promptly toward Reims to crown the Dauphin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage169_169‚Äì170]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage56_157]Richey2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcwarrior0000richpage66_66]_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage169_169‚Äì170]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage56_157]Richey2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcwarrior0000richpage66_66]-127">[123]</a></sup> Charles allowed her to accompany the army under the command of <a href="/wiki/John_II,_Duke_of_Alen%C3%A7on" title="John II, Duke of Alen√ßon">John II, Duke of Alen√ßon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage128_128]Richey2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcwarrior0000richpage66_66]_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage128_128]Richey2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcwarrior0000richpage66_66]-128">[124]</a></sup> who collaboratively worked with Joan and regularly heeded her advice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page102_102]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage90_90]_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page102_102]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage90_90]-129">[125]</a></sup> Before advancing toward Reims, the Armagnacs needed to recapture the bridge towns along the Loire: <a href="/wiki/Jargeau" title="Jargeau">Jargeau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meung-sur-Loire" title="Meung-sur-Loire">Meung-sur-Loire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Beaugency" title="Beaugency">Beaugency</a>. This would clear the way for Charles and his entourage, who would have to cross the Loire near Orl√©ans to get from Chinon to Reims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page114_114]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage127_127‚Äì128]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen132_116]_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page114_114]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage127_127‚Äì128]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen132_116]-130">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Loire_Campaign_(1429)" title="Loire Campaign (1429)">campaign to clear the Loire towns</a> began on 11 June when the Armagnac forces led by Alen√ßon and Joan arrived at Jargeau<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page101_101]Barker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage120_120]_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page101_101]Barker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage120_120]-131">[127]</a></sup> and forced the English to withdraw inside the town's walls. Joan sent a message to the English to surrender; they refused<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage250_250]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page104_104]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage131_131]_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage250_250]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page104_104]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage131_131]-132">[128]</a></sup> and she advocated for a direct assault on the walls the next day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage250_250]Castor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page115_115]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page105_105]_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage250_250]Castor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page115_115]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page105_105]-133">[129]</a></sup> By the end of the day, the town was taken. The Armagnac took few prisoners and many of the English who surrendered were killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage120_120‚Äì121]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page104_104]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage132_132]_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage120_120‚Äì121]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page104_104]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage132_132]-134">[130]</a></sup> During this campaign, Joan continued to serve in the thick of battle. She began scaling a siege ladder with her banner in hand but before she could climb the wall, she was struck by a stone which split her helmet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage93_93]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen141_126]_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage93_93]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen141_126]-135">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alen√ßon and Joan's army advanced on <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Meung-sur-Loire" title="Battle of Meung-sur-Loire">Meung-sur-Loire</a>. On 15 June, they took control of the town's bridge, and the English garrison withdrew to a castle on the Loire's north bank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage252_252]_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage252_252]-136">[132]</a></sup> Most of the army continued on the south bank of the Loire to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Beaugency_(1429)" title="Battle of Beaugency (1429)">besiege the castle at Beaugency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage121_121]Burne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage252_252]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage94_94‚Äì91]_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage121_121]Burne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage252_252]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage94_94‚Äì91]-137">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, the English army from Paris under the command of Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Fastolf" title="John Fastolf">John Fastolf</a> had linked up with the garrison in Meung and traveled along the north bank of the Loire to relieve Beaugency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage122_122]Burne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage253_253‚Äì254]_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage122_122]Burne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage253_253‚Äì254]-138">[134]</a></sup> Unaware of this, the English garrison at Beaugency surrendered on 18 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage122_122]_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage122_122]-139">[135]</a></sup> The main English army retreated toward Paris; Joan urged the Armagnacs to pursue them, and the two armies clashed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Patay" title="Battle of Patay">Battle of Patay</a> later that day. The English had prepared their forces to ambush an Armagnac attack with hidden <a href="/wiki/English_longbow" title="English longbow">archers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page118_118]_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page118_118]-140">[136]</a></sup> but the Armagnac vanguard detected and scattered them. A rout ensued that decimated the English army. Fastolf escaped with a small band of soldiers, but many of the English leaders were captured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage98_98]_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage98_98]-141">[137]</a></sup> Joan arrived at the battlefield too late to participate in the decisive action,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page120_120]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage98_98]_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page120_120]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage98_98]-142">[138]</a></sup> but her encouragement to pursue the English had made the victory possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage256_256]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage100_100]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage176_176‚Äì177]Richey2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcwarrior0000richpage75_75]_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage256_256]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage100_100]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage176_176‚Äì177]Richey2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcwarrior0000richpage75_75]-143">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coronation_and_siege_of_Paris">Coronation and siege of Paris</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BNF_NAF_4811,_folio_55_verso_-_couronnement_de_Charles_VII.jpg"><img alt="Miniature of coronation of King Charles the seventh of France" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1302" data-file-width="1216" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/BNF_NAF_4811%2C_folio_55_verso_-_couronnement_de_Charles_VII.jpg/190px-BNF_NAF_4811%2C_folio_55_verso_-_couronnement_de_Charles_VII.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/BNF_NAF_4811%2C_folio_55_verso_-_couronnement_de_Charles_VII.jpg/285px-BNF_NAF_4811%2C_folio_55_verso_-_couronnement_de_Charles_VII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/BNF_NAF_4811%2C_folio_55_verso_-_couronnement_de_Charles_VII.jpg/380px-BNF_NAF_4811%2C_folio_55_verso_-_couronnement_de_Charles_VII.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BNF_NAF_4811,_folio_55_verso_-_couronnement_de_Charles_VII.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coronation of <a href="/wiki/Charles_VII_of_France" title="Charles VII of France">Charles VII</a> in Philippe Contamine, <i>Charles VII¬†: une vie, une politique</i>; Joan of Arc stands holding a banner of France to his right. Unknown author (15th century).</div></div></div>
<p>After the destruction of the English army at Patay, some Armagnac leaders argued for an invasion of English-held Normandy, but Joan remained insistent that Charles must be crowned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage126_126]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage101_101‚Äì103],_[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage105_105]_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage126_126]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage101_101‚Äì103],_[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage105_105]-144">[140]</a></sup> The Dauphin agreed, and the army left <a href="/wiki/Gien" title="Gien">Gien</a> on 29 June to <a href="/wiki/March_to_Reims" title="March to Reims">march on Reims</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichelet1855[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcmaidofo00michpage86_86‚Äì87]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage62_62]_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichelet1855[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcmaidofo00michpage86_86‚Äì87]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage62_62]-145">[141]</a></sup> The advance was nearly unopposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage126_126]Burne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage261_261]_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage126_126]Burne1956[httpsarchiveorgdetailsagincourtwarmili0000burnpage261_261]-146">[142]</a></sup> The Burgundian-held town of <a href="/wiki/Auxerre" title="Auxerre">Auxerre</a> surrendered on 3 July after three days of negotiations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page128_128]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage106_106]_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page128_128]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage106_106]-147">[143]</a></sup> and other towns in the army's path returned to Armagnac allegiance without resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage126_126]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page130_130]_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage126_126]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page130_130]-148">[144]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Troyes" title="Troyes">Troyes</a>, which had a small garrison of English and Burgundian troops,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page130_130]Michelet1855[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcmaidofo00michpage87_87]_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page130_130]Michelet1855[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcmaidofo00michpage87_87]-149">[145]</a></sup> was the only one to resist. After four days of negotiation, Joan ordered the soldiers to fill the city's moat with wood and directed the placement of artillery. Fearing an assault, Troyes negotiated a surrender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page130_130]Michelet1855[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcmaidofo00michpage89_89‚Äì90]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage63_63]_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page130_130]Michelet1855[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcmaidofo00michpage89_89‚Äì90]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage63_63]-150">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reims opened its gates on 16 July 1429. Charles, Joan, and the army entered in the evening, and Charles's consecration took place the following morning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page133_133]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage159_159]_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page133_133]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage159_159]-151">[147]</a></sup> Joan was given a place of honor at the ceremony,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage126_126]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage162_162]_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage126_126]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage162_162]-152">[148]</a></sup> and announced that God's will had been fulfilled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page134_134]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage112_112]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage66_66]_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page134_134]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage112_112]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage66_66]-153">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the consecration, the royal court negotiated a truce of fifteen days with the Duke of Burgundy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage72_72]_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage72_72]-154">[150]</a></sup> who promised he would try to arrange the transfer of Paris to the Armagnacs while continuing negotiations for a definitive peace. At the end of the truce, Burgundy reneged on his promise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page140_140]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen179_163‚Äì164]_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page140_140]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen179_163‚Äì164]-155">[151]</a></sup>  Joan and the Duke of Alen√ßon favored a quick march on Paris,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage128_128]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage72_72]Richey2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcwarrior0000richpage78_78]_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage128_128]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage72_72]Richey2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcwarrior0000richpage78_78]-156">[152]</a></sup> but divisions in Charles's court and continued peace negotiations with Burgundy led to a slow advance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page147_147]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage200_200‚Äì201]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen179_163‚Äì164]_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page147_147]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage200_200‚Äì201]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen179_163‚Äì164]-157">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the Armagnac army approached Paris, many of the towns along the way surrendered without a fight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage130_130]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page142_142]_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage130_130]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page142_142]-158">[154]</a></sup> On 15 August, the English forces under the Duke of Bedford confronted the Armagnacs near <a href="/wiki/Mont%C3%A9pilloy" title="Mont√©pilloy">Mont√©pilloy</a> in a fortified position that the Armagnac commanders thought was too strong to assault. Joan rode out in front of the English positions to try to provoke them to attack. They refused, resulting in a standoff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage132_132]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page142_142‚Äì143]_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage132_132]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page142_142‚Äì143]-159">[155]</a></sup> The English retreated the following day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page144_144]_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page144_144]-160">[156]</a></sup> The Armagnacs continued their advance and launched an <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Paris_(1429)" title="Siege of Paris (1429)">assault on Paris</a> on 8 September.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage134_134]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page150_150]_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage134_134]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page150_150]-161">[157]</a></sup> During the fighting, Joan was wounded in the leg by a crossbow bolt. She remained in a trench beneath the city walls until she was rescued after nightfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage136_136]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage77_77]_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage136_136]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage77_77]-162">[158]</a></sup> The Armagnacs had suffered 1,500 casualties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage136_136]_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage136_136]-163">[159]</a></sup> The following morning, Charles ordered an end to the assault. Joan was displeased<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page152_152‚Äì153]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage78_78]_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page152_152‚Äì153]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage78_78]-164">[160]</a></sup> and argued that the attack should be continued. She and Alen√ßon had made fresh plans to attack Paris, but Charles dismantled a bridge approaching Paris that was necessary for the attack and the Armagnac army had to retreat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page152_152]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage126_126]_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page152_152]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage126_126]-165">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the defeat at Paris, Joan's role in the French court diminished. Her aggressive independence did not agree with the court's emphasis on finding a diplomatic solution with Burgundy, and her role in the defeat at Paris reduced the court's faith in her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page156_156]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage130_130]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage209_209]_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page156_156]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage130_130]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage209_209]-166">[162]</a></sup>
After the defeat, scholars at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a> argued that she failed to take Paris because her inspiration was not divine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page143_143]_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page143_143]-167">[163]</a></sup> In September, Charles disbanded the army, and Joan was not allowed to work with the Duke of Alen√ßon again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage136_136‚Äì137]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page153_153]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage78_78‚Äì79]_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage136_136‚Äì137]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page153_153]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage78_78‚Äì79]-168">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_against_Perrinet_Gressart">Campaign against Perrinet Gressart</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jeanne_d%27Arc_-_Les_vies_des_femmes_c%C3%A9l%C3%A8bres.jpg"><img alt="A human figure on horseback, with the horse pointing left. The figure is wearing armor and carrying an orange banner. The horse is white and has red accessories." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1619" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Jeanne_d%27Arc_-_Les_vies_des_femmes_c%C3%A9l%C3%A8bres.jpg/190px-Jeanne_d%27Arc_-_Les_vies_des_femmes_c%C3%A9l%C3%A8bres.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Jeanne_d%27Arc_-_Les_vies_des_femmes_c%C3%A9l%C3%A8bres.jpg/285px-Jeanne_d%27Arc_-_Les_vies_des_femmes_c%C3%A9l%C3%A8bres.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Jeanne_d%27Arc_-_Les_vies_des_femmes_c%C3%A9l%C3%A8bres.jpg/380px-Jeanne_d%27Arc_-_Les_vies_des_femmes_c%C3%A9l%C3%A8bres.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jeanne_d%27Arc_-_Les_vies_des_femmes_c%C3%A9l%C3%A8bres.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Miniature depicting Jeanne d'Arc from <i>The Lives of Famous Women</i>, by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jean_Pichore&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jean Pichore (page does not exist)">Jean Pichore</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">¬†[<a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean_Pichore" title="fr:Jean Pichore">fr</a>]</span> (1506, <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Dobr%C3%A9e" title="Mus√©e Dobr√©e">Mus√©e Dobr√©e</a>, Nantes, France)</div></div></div>
<p>In October, Joan was sent as part of a force to attack the territory of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Perrinet_Gressart&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Perrinet Gressart (page does not exist)">Perrinet Gressart</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">¬†[<a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perrinet_Gressart" title="fr:Perrinet Gressart">fr</a>]</span>, a mercenary who had served the Burgundians and English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage137_137‚Äì138]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page157_157]_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage137_137‚Äì138]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page157_157]-169">[165]</a></sup> The army <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Saint-Pierre-le-Mo%C3%BBtier" title="Siege of Saint-Pierre-le-Mo√ªtier">besieged Saint-Pierre-le-Mo√ªtier</a>, which fell after Joan encouraged a direct assault on 4 November. The army then tried unsuccessfully to take <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_La_Charit%C3%A9" title="Siege of La Charit√©">La-Charit√©-sur-Loire</a> in November and December and had to abandon their artillery during the retreat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page157_157]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage81_81]_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page157_157]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage81_81]-170">[166]</a></sup> This defeat further diminished Joan's reputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage137_137]Castor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page149_149]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen207_191]Richey2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcwarrior0000richpage81_81]_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage137_137]Castor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page149_149]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen207_191]Richey2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcwarrior0000richpage81_81]-171">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>Joan returned to court at the end of December,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage134_134]_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage134_134]-172">[168]</a></sup> where she learned that she and her family had been ennobled by Charles as a reward for her services to him and the kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage193_193]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage81_81]_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage193_193]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage81_81]-173">[169]</a></sup> Before the September attack on Paris, Charles had negotiated a four-month truce with the Burgundians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage132_132]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page145_145]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage174_174]_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage132_132]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page145_145]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage174_174]-174">[170]</a></sup> which was extended until Easter 1430.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELang1909[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmaidoffrancebein00languoftpage199_199]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen209_193]_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELang1909[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmaidoffrancebein00languoftpage199_199]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen209_193]-175">[171]</a></sup> During this truce, the French court had no need for Joan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage138_138]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page165_165]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage212_212‚Äì214]_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage138_138]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page165_165]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage212_212‚Äì214]-176">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Siege_of_Compi.C3.A8gne_and_capture"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Siege_of_Compi√®gne_and_capture">Siege of Compi√®gne and capture</span></h3>
<p>The Duke of Burgundy began to reclaim towns which had been ceded to him by treaty but had not submitted to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage84_84]_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage84_84]-177">[173]</a></sup> Compi√®gne was one such town<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage146_146]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page167_167‚Äì168]_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage146_146]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page167_167‚Äì168]-178">[174]</a></sup> of many in areas which the Armagnacs had recaptured over the previous few months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page166_166]_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page166_166]-179">[175]</a></sup> Joan set out with a company of volunteers at the end of March 1430 to relieve the town, which was under siege.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page168_168]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage136_136]_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page168_168]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage136_136]-180">[176]</a></sup> This expedition did not have the explicit permission of Charles, who was still observing the truce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELang1909[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmaidoffrancebein00languoftpage226_226]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage84_84‚Äì85]Vale1974_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELang1909[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmaidoffrancebein00languoftpage226_226]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage84_84‚Äì85]Vale1974-181">[177]</a></sup> Some writers suggest that Joan's expedition to Compi√®gne without documented permission from the court was a desperate and treasonable action,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage146_146]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page168_168]_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage146_146]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page168_168]-182">[178]</a></sup> but others have argued that she could not have launched the expedition without the financial support of the court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage136_136]Lightbody1961[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjudgementsofjoan0000lighpage152_152]_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage136_136]Lightbody1961[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjudgementsofjoan0000lighpage152_152]-183">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April, Joan arrived at <a href="/wiki/Melun" title="Melun">Melun</a>, which had expelled its Burgundian garrison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage136_136]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage84_85]_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage136_136]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage84_85]-184">[180]</a></sup> As Joan advanced, her force grew as other commanders joined her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page168_168‚Äì169]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage86_86]_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page168_168‚Äì169]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage86_86]-185">[181]</a></sup> Joan's troops advanced to <a href="/wiki/Lagny-sur-Marne" title="Lagny-sur-Marne">Lagny-sur-Marne</a> and defeated an Anglo-Burgundian force commanded by the mercenary Franquet d'Arras who was captured. Typically, he would have been ransomed or exchanged by the capturing force, but Joan allowed the townspeople to execute him after a trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page169_169]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage137_137‚Äì138]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage85_85]_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page169_169]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage137_137‚Äì138]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage85_85]-186">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panth%C3%A9on_-_La_vie_de_Jeanne_d%27Arc_(hlw16_0310)-_crop_capture_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="Joan in armor and surcoat being pulled off her horse by soldiers." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="1519" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Panth%C3%A9on_-_La_vie_de_Jeanne_d%27Arc_%28hlw16_0310%29-_crop_capture_%28cropped%29.jpg/320px-Panth%C3%A9on_-_La_vie_de_Jeanne_d%27Arc_%28hlw16_0310%29-_crop_capture_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Panth%C3%A9on_-_La_vie_de_Jeanne_d%27Arc_%28hlw16_0310%29-_crop_capture_%28cropped%29.jpg/480px-Panth%C3%A9on_-_La_vie_de_Jeanne_d%27Arc_%28hlw16_0310%29-_crop_capture_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Panth%C3%A9on_-_La_vie_de_Jeanne_d%27Arc_%28hlw16_0310%29-_crop_capture_%28cropped%29.jpg/640px-Panth%C3%A9on_-_La_vie_de_Jeanne_d%27Arc_%28hlw16_0310%29-_crop_capture_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Panth%C3%A9on_-_La_vie_de_Jeanne_d%27Arc_(hlw16_0310)-_crop_capture_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mural <i>Joan captured by the Burgundians at Compi√®gne</i> by <a href="/wiki/Jules-Eug%C3%A8ne_Lenepveu" title="Jules-Eug√®ne Lenepveu">Jules-Eug√®ne Lenepveu</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>¬†1886‚Äì1890, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panth%C3%A9on,_Paris" title="Panth√©on, Paris">Panth√©on</a>, Paris)</div></div></div>
<p>Joan reached Compi√®gne on 14 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page169_169]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage138_138]_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page169_169]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage138_138]-187">[183]</a></sup> After defensive forays against the Burgundian besiegers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page171_171]_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page171_171]-188">[184]</a></sup> she was forced to disband the majority of the army because it had become too difficult for the surrounding countryside to support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage139_139]_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage139_139]-189">[185]</a></sup> Joan and about 400 of her remaining soldiers entered the town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage86_86]_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage86_86]-190">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 23 May 1430, Joan accompanied an Armagnac force which <a href="/wiki/Sortie" title="Sortie">sortied</a> from Compi√®gne to attack the Burgundian camp at <a href="/wiki/Margny-l%C3%A8s-Compi%C3%A8gne" title="Margny-l√®s-Compi√®gne">Margny</a>, northeast of the town. The attack failed, and Joan was captured;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage146_146]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page174_174‚Äì177]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage227_227‚Äì228]_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker2009[httpsarchiveorgdetailsconquestenglishk0000barkpage146_146]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page174_174‚Äì177]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage227_227‚Äì228]-191">[187]</a></sup> She agreed to surrender to a pro-Burgundian nobleman named Lyonnel de Wandomme, a member of <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Luxembourg,_Count_of_Ligny" title="John II of Luxembourg, Count of Ligny">Jean de Luxembourg</a>'s contingent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage88_88]_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage88_88]-192">[188]</a></sup> who quickly moved her to his castle at <a href="/wiki/Beaulieu-les-Fontaines" title="Beaulieu-les-Fontaines">Beaulieu-les-Fontaines</a> near <a href="/wiki/Noyes" title="Noyes">Noyes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage142_142]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage92_92]_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage142_142]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage92_92]-193">[189]</a></sup> After her first attempt to escape, she was transferred to <a href="/wiki/Beaurevoir" title="Beaurevoir">Beaurevoir</a> Castle. She made another escape attempt while there, jumping from a window of a 70-foot (21¬†m) tower and landing in a dry moat; she was injured but survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page163_163]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage149_149]Warner1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcimageof0000warnpage113_113]_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page163_163]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage149_149]Warner1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcimageof0000warnpage113_113]-194">[190]</a></sup> In November, she was moved to the Burgundian town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arras,_France" title="Arras, France">Arras</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage97_97]_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage97_97]-195">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>The English and Burgundians rejoiced that Joan had been removed as a military threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERankinQuintal1964[httpsarchiveorgdetailsfirstbiographyof0000rankpage111_111‚Äì112]_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERankinQuintal1964[httpsarchiveorgdetailsfirstbiographyof0000rankpage111_111‚Äì112]-196">[192]</a></sup> The English negotiated with their Burgundian allies to pay Joan's ransom and transfer her to their custody. Bishop <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Cauchon" title="Pierre Cauchon">Pierre Cauchon</a> of <a href="/wiki/Beauvais" title="Beauvais">Beauvais</a>, a partisan supporter of the Duke of Burgundy and the English crown,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChampion1920[httpswebarchiveorgweb20160818165959httpssourcebooksfordhamedubasisjoanofarc-trialasp_405]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage208_208‚Äì209]_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChampion1920[httpswebarchiveorgweb20160818165959httpssourcebooksfordhamedubasisjoanofarc-trialasp_405]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage208_208‚Äì209]-197">[193]</a></sup> played a prominent part in these negotiations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page162_162‚Äì163]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage210_210‚Äì211]_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page162_162‚Äì163]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage210_210‚Äì211]-198">[194]</a></sup> which were completed in November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage22_22]_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage22_22]-199">[195]</a></sup> The final agreement called for the English to pay 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Livre_tournois" title="Livre tournois">livres tournois</a> to obtain her from Luxembourg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page183_183]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage97_97]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage212_212]_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page183_183]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage97_97]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage212_212]-200">[196]</a></sup> After the English paid the ransom, they moved Joan to <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a>, their main headquarters in France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page164_164]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage100_100‚Äì101]_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page164_164]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage100_100‚Äì101]-201">[197]</a></sup> There is no evidence that Charles tried to save Joan once she was transferred to the English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage143_143‚Äì144]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page168_168]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage97_97‚Äì98]Vale1974[httpsarchiveorgdetailscharlesvii0000valepage58_58‚Äì59]_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage143_143‚Äì144]DeVries1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsisbn_9780750918053page168_168]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage97_97‚Äì98]Vale1974[httpsarchiveorgdetailscharlesvii0000valepage58_58‚Äì59]-202">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trials_and_execution">Trials and execution</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trial">Trial</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trial_of_Joan_of_Arc" title="Trial of Joan of Arc">Trial of Joan of Arc</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Maurice_Boutet_de_Monvel,_The_Trial_of_Joan_of_Arc_(Joan_of_Arc_series_-_VI),_c._late_1909-early_1910,_NGA_195567.jpg"><img alt="Joan of Arc facing left addressing assessors, scribes. She has soldiers behind her" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1765" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Louis_Maurice_Boutet_de_Monvel%2C_The_Trial_of_Joan_of_Arc_%28Joan_of_Arc_series_-_VI%29%2C_c._late_1909-early_1910%2C_NGA_195567.jpg/330px-Louis_Maurice_Boutet_de_Monvel%2C_The_Trial_of_Joan_of_Arc_%28Joan_of_Arc_series_-_VI%29%2C_c._late_1909-early_1910%2C_NGA_195567.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Louis_Maurice_Boutet_de_Monvel%2C_The_Trial_of_Joan_of_Arc_%28Joan_of_Arc_series_-_VI%29%2C_c._late_1909-early_1910%2C_NGA_195567.jpg/495px-Louis_Maurice_Boutet_de_Monvel%2C_The_Trial_of_Joan_of_Arc_%28Joan_of_Arc_series_-_VI%29%2C_c._late_1909-early_1910%2C_NGA_195567.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Louis_Maurice_Boutet_de_Monvel%2C_The_Trial_of_Joan_of_Arc_%28Joan_of_Arc_series_-_VI%29%2C_c._late_1909-early_1910%2C_NGA_195567.jpg/660px-Louis_Maurice_Boutet_de_Monvel%2C_The_Trial_of_Joan_of_Arc_%28Joan_of_Arc_series_-_VI%29%2C_c._late_1909-early_1910%2C_NGA_195567.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Maurice_Boutet_de_Monvel,_The_Trial_of_Joan_of_Arc_(Joan_of_Arc_series_-_VI),_c._late_1909-early_1910,_NGA_195567.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Trial of Joan of Arc</i>, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_Maurice_Boutet_de_Monvel" title="Louis Maurice Boutet de Monvel">Louis Maurice Boutet de Monvel</a> (1909‚Äì1910, <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Art" title="National Gallery of Art">National Gallery of Art</a>, Washington, D.C.)</div></div></div>
<p>Joan was put on trial for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_heresy" title="Christian heresy">heresy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage14_14‚Äì15]Sullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpagen14_xviii]Russell1972[httpsarchiveorgdetailswitchcraftinmidd0000russpage262_262]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage22_22]_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage14_14‚Äì15]Sullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpagen14_xviii]Russell1972[httpsarchiveorgdetailswitchcraftinmidd0000russpage262_262]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage22_22]-203">[199]</a></sup> in Rouen on 9 January 1431.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage24_24]_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage24_24]-204">[200]</a></sup>
She was accused of having <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy" title="Blasphemy">blasphemed</a> by wearing men's clothes, of acting upon visions that were <a href="/wiki/Demon" title="Demon">demonic</a>, and of refusing to submit her words and deeds to the church because she claimed she would be judged by God alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage207_207‚Äì208]_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage207_207‚Äì208]-205">[201]</a></sup> Joan's captors downplayed the secular aspects of her trial by submitting her judgment to an ecclesiastical court, but the trial was politically motivated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeters1989[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidTnqLow3iKd4CpgPA69_69]Weiskopf1996[httpsarchiveorgdetailsfreshverdictsonj0000unsepage118_118]_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeters1989[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidTnqLow3iKd4CpgPA69_69]Weiskopf1996[httpsarchiveorgdetailsfreshverdictsonj0000unsepage118_118]-206">[202]</a></sup>  Joan testified that her visions had instructed her to defeat the English and crown Charles, and her success was argued to be evidence she was acting on behalf of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott2002[httpswwwjstororgstable101086532095seq21_46‚Äì47]_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott2002[httpswwwjstororgstable101086532095seq21_46‚Äì47]-207">[203]</a></sup> If unchallenged, her testimony would invalidate the English claim to the rule of France<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage20_20]_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage20_20]-208">[204]</a></sup> and undermine the University of Paris,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage146_146‚Äì147]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage107_107]_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage146_146‚Äì147]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage107_107]-209">[205]</a></sup> which supported the dual monarchy ruled by an English king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage4_4]Hobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage3_3]Verger1972[httpsarchiveorgdetailsuniversitiesinpo0000unsepage53_53‚Äì54]_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage4_4]Hobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage3_3]Verger1972[httpsarchiveorgdetailsuniversitiesinpo0000unsepage53_53‚Äì54]-210">[206]</a></sup>
</p><p>The verdict was a foregone conclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage8_8]Kelly1993[httpswwwjstororgstablepdf2865494seq33_1023‚Äì1024]Sullivan2011[httpswwwjstororgstable23347178seq7_313]_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage8_8]Kelly1993[httpswwwjstororgstablepdf2865494seq33_1023‚Äì1024]Sullivan2011[httpswwwjstororgstable23347178seq7_313]-211">[207]</a></sup>
Joan's guilt could be used to compromise Charles's claims to legitimacy by showing that he had been consecrated by the act of a heretic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage20_20‚Äì21]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage106_106]_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage20_20‚Äì21]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage106_106]-212">[208]</a></sup> Cauchon served as the <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_jurisdiction#Ordinary" title="Ecclesiastical jurisdiction">ordinary</a> judge of the trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELightbody1961[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjudgementsofjoan0000lighpage102_102]_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELightbody1961[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjudgementsofjoan0000lighpage102_102]-213">[209]</a></sup> The English subsidized the trial,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpagen13_xiii]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage156_156]Lightbody1961[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjudgementsofjoan0000lighpage102_102‚Äì103]_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpagen13_xiii]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage156_156]Lightbody1961[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjudgementsofjoan0000lighpage102_102‚Äì103]-214">[210]</a></sup> including payments to Cauchon<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewhall1934[httpswwwjstororgstable2846456seq2_89]Warner1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcimageof0000warnpage47_47]_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewhall1934[httpswwwjstororgstable2846456seq2_89]Warner1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcimageof0000warnpage47_47]-215">[211]</a></sup> and Jean Le Ma√Ætre,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage214_214]_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage214_214]-216">[212]</a></sup> who represented the Inquisitor of France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage156_156]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage23_23]_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage156_156]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage23_23]-217">[213]</a></sup> All but 8 of the 131 clergy who participated in the trial were French<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage4_4]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage23_23]_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage4_4]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage23_23]-218">[214]</a></sup> and two thirds were associated with the University of Paris,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage253_253]Hobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage23_23]_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage253_253]Hobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage23_23]-219">[215]</a></sup> but most were pro-Burgundian and pro-English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPernoud1962[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcbyherse00pernpage166_166]Warner1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcimageof0000warnpage48_48]_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPernoud1962[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcbyherse00pernpage166_166]Warner1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcimageof0000warnpage48_48]-220">[216]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_Cauchon-Jeanne_Darc_manuscript.jpg"><img alt="miniature of Pierre Couchon" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="931" data-file-width="1078" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Pierre_Cauchon-Jeanne_Darc_manuscript.jpg/260px-Pierre_Cauchon-Jeanne_Darc_manuscript.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Pierre_Cauchon-Jeanne_Darc_manuscript.jpg/390px-Pierre_Cauchon-Jeanne_Darc_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Pierre_Cauchon-Jeanne_Darc_manuscript.jpg/520px-Pierre_Cauchon-Jeanne_Darc_manuscript.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_Cauchon-Jeanne_Darc_manuscript.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Miniature of <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Cauchon" title="Pierre Cauchon">Pierre Cauchon</a> presiding at Joan of Arc's trial, unknown author (15th century, <a href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale_de_France" title="Biblioth√®que nationale de France">Biblioth√®que nationale de France</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Cauchon attempted to follow correct inquisitorial procedure,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage18_18]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage108_108]Sullivan2011[httpswwwjstororgstable23347178seq5_311]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage29_29]_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage18_18]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage108_108]Sullivan2011[httpswwwjstororgstable23347178seq5_311]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage29_29]-221">[217]</a></sup> but the trial had many irregularities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage156_157]Hobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage7_7]Peters1989[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidTnqLow3iKd4CpgPA69_69]_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage156_157]Hobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage7_7]Peters1989[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidTnqLow3iKd4CpgPA69_69]-222">[218]</a></sup> Joan should have been in the hands of the church during the trial and guarded by women,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage26_26]_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage26_26]-223">[219]</a></sup> but instead was imprisoned by the English and guarded by male soldiers under the command of the Duke of Bedford.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage154_154]_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage154_154]-224">[220]</a></sup> Contrary to <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a>, Cauchon had not established Joan's <a href="/wiki/Infamy#Infamy_of_law" title="Infamy">infamy</a> before proceeding with the trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage254_234‚Äì255]Kelly1993[httpswwwjstororgstablepdf2865494seq28_1018],_[httpswwwjstororgstablepdf2865494seq32_1022]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage24_24‚Äì25]_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage254_234‚Äì255]Kelly1993[httpswwwjstororgstablepdf2865494seq28_1018],_[httpswwwjstororgstablepdf2865494seq32_1022]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage24_24‚Äì25]-225">[221]</a></sup>
Joan was not read the charges against her until well after her interrogations began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly1993[httpswwwjstororgstablepdf2865494seq32_1022]_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly1993[httpswwwjstororgstablepdf2865494seq32_1022]-226">[222]</a></sup> The procedures were below inquisitorial standards,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeters1989[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidTnqLow3iKd4CpgPA69_69]_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeters1989[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidTnqLow3iKd4CpgPA69_69]-227">[223]</a></sup> subjecting Joan to lengthy interrogations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage88_88‚Äì89]_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage88_88‚Äì89]-228">[224]</a></sup> without legal counsel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage7_7]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage25_25,_fn_79]_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage7_7]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage25_25,_fn_79]-229">[225]</a></sup> One of the trial clerics stepped down because he felt the testimony was coerced and its intention was to entrap Joan;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrank1997[httpswwwjstororgstable29759909seq4_54]Kelly1993[httpswwwjstororgstable2865494seq28_1018]_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrank1997[httpswwwjstororgstable29759909seq4_54]Kelly1993[httpswwwjstororgstable2865494seq28_1018]-230">[226]</a></sup> another challenged Cauchon's right to judge the trial and was jailed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrank1997[httpswwwjstororgstable29759909seq4_54]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage156_156‚Äì157]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage126_126]_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrank1997[httpswwwjstororgstable29759909seq4_54]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage156_156‚Äì157]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage126_126]-231">[227]</a></sup> There is evidence that the trial records were falsified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage7_7]RankinQuintal1964[httpsarchiveorgdetailsfirstbiographyof0000rankpage113_101]_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage7_7]RankinQuintal1964[httpsarchiveorgdetailsfirstbiographyof0000rankpage113_101]-232">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the trial, Joan showed great control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage160_160]Taylor2009[httpswwwgooglecombookseditionThe_Virgin_WarriorsV9kFj3O1noChlengbpv1bsq22Joan27s20knowledge20of20tactics22_160]_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage160_160]Taylor2009[httpswwwgooglecombookseditionThe_Virgin_WarriorsV9kFj3O1noChlengbpv1bsq22Joan27s20knowledge20of20tactics22_160]-233">[229]</a></sup> She induced her interrogators to ask questions sequentially rather than simultaneously, refer back to their records when appropriate, and end the sessions when she requested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage102_102]_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage102_102]-234">[230]</a></sup> Witnesses at the trial were impressed by her prudence when answering questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage160_160]Sullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage102_102]_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage160_160]Sullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage102_102]-235">[231]</a></sup> For example, in one exchange she was asked if she knew she was in God's grace. The question was meant as a scholarly trap, as church doctrine held that nobody could be certain of being in God's grace. If she answered positively, she would have been charged with heresy; if negatively, she would have confessed her own guilt. Joan avoided the trap by stating that if she was not in God's grace, she hoped God would put her there, and if she was in God's grace then she hoped she would remain so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarstow1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcheretic0000barspage92_93]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage166_166]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage112_112]_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarstow1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcheretic0000barspage92_93]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage166_166]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage112_112]-236">[232]</a></sup> One of the court notaries at her trial later testified that the interrogators were stunned by her answer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage166_166]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage238_238]_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage166_166]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage238_238]-237">[233]</a></sup> To convince her to submit, Joan was shown the instruments of torture. When she refused to be intimidated, Cauchon met with about a dozen assessors (clerical jurors) to vote on whether she should be tortured. The majority decided against it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage206_206]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage127_127‚Äì128]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage256_256]_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage206_206]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage127_127‚Äì128]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage256_256]-238">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>In early May, Cauchon asked the University of Paris to deliberate on twelve articles summarizing the accusation of heresy. The university approved the charges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage208_208‚Äì209]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage288_288]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage129_129]_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage208_208‚Äì209]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage288_288]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage129_129]-239">[235]</a></sup> On 23 May, Joan was formally admonished by the court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page186_186]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen335_318]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage129_129]_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page186_186]Lowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen335_318]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage129_129]-240">[236]</a></sup> The next day, she was taken out to the churchyard of the abbey of Saint-Ouen for public condemnation. As Cauchon began to read Joan's sentence, she agreed to submit. She was presented with an <a href="/wiki/Abjuration" title="Abjuration">abjuration</a> document, which included an agreement that she would not bear arms or wear men's clothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage212_212]_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage212_212]-241">[237]</a></sup> It was read aloud to her,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page190_190]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage214_214]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage131_131]_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page190_190]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage214_214]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage131_131]-242">[238]</a></sup> and she signed it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarstow1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcheretic0000barspage115_115‚Äì116]Castor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page190_190]Sullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage131_131]_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarstow1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcheretic0000barspage115_115‚Äì116]Castor2015[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarchistory0000cast_n6r7page190_190]Sullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage131_131]-243">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[e]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Execution">Execution</span></h3>
<p>Public heresy was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capital_crime" title="Capital crime">capital crime</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMegivern1997[httpsarchiveorgdetailsdeathpenaltyhist1997megipage128_128]_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMegivern1997[httpsarchiveorgdetailsdeathpenaltyhist1997megipage128_128]-247">[242]</a></sup> in which an unrepentant or relapsed heretic could be given over to the judgment of the secular courts and punished by death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoonan1998[httpswwwjstororgstable25025339_703]_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoonan1998[httpswwwjstororgstable25025339_703]-248">[243]</a></sup> Having signed the abjuration, Joan was no longer an unrepentant heretic, but could be executed if convicted of relapsing into heresy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2014[httpswwwjstororgstable43577195seq14_949]Noonan1987[httpsjstororgstable1051025seq3_204‚Äì205]_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2014[httpswwwjstororgstable43577195seq14_949]Noonan1987[httpsjstororgstable1051025seq3_204‚Äì205]-249">[244]</a></sup>
</p><p>As part of her abjuration, Joan was required to renounce wearing men's clothes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoonan1987[httpswwwjstororgstable1051025_203]_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoonan1987[httpswwwjstororgstable1051025_203]-250">[245]</a></sup> She exchanged her clothes for a woman's dress and allowed her head to be shaved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchibanoff1996[httpsarchiveorgdetailsfreshverdictsonj0000unsepage37_37]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage271_271]_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchibanoff1996[httpsarchiveorgdetailsfreshverdictsonj0000unsepage37_37]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage271_271]-251">[246]</a></sup> She was returned to her cell and kept in chains<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHotchkiss2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidfrPPPIJvUDgCpgPA64_64‚Äì65]_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHotchkiss2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidfrPPPIJvUDgCpgPA64_64‚Äì65]-252">[247]</a></sup> instead of being transferred to an ecclesiastical prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELightbody1961[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjudgementsofjoan0000lighpage138_138_fn3]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage269_269]_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELightbody1961[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjudgementsofjoan0000lighpage138_138_fn3]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage269_269]-253">[248]</a></sup>
Witnesses at the rehabilitation trial stated that Joan was subjected to mistreatment and rape attempts, including one by an English noble,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrane1996[httpswebarchiveorgweb20160705094811httpsfacultysmuedubwheelerjoan_of_arcolr08_clothingjoanofarc_cranepdf_302‚Äì303]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage216_216]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage273_273]Michelet1855[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcmaidofo00michpage222_222]_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrane1996[httpswebarchiveorgweb20160705094811httpsfacultysmuedubwheelerjoan_of_arcolr08_clothingjoanofarc_cranepdf_302‚Äì303]Gies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage216_216]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage273_273]Michelet1855[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcmaidofo00michpage222_222]-254">[249]</a></sup> and that guards placed men's clothes in her cell, forcing her to wear them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHotchkiss2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidfrPPPIJvUDgCpgPA66_66]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage272_272]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage132_132]_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHotchkiss2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidfrPPPIJvUDgCpgPA66_66]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage272_272]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage132_132]-255">[250]</a></sup> Cauchon was notified that Joan had resumed wearing male clothing. He sent clerics to admonish her to remain in submission, but the English prevented them from visiting her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen347_329]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage273_273]_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELowell1896[httpsarchiveorgdetailsJoanOfArc1896pagen347_329]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage273_273]-256">[251]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vigiles_du_roi_Charles_VII_10.jpg"><img alt="Joan in red dress being bound to a stake as a group of men look on" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1642" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Vigiles_du_roi_Charles_VII_10.jpg/290px-Vigiles_du_roi_Charles_VII_10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Vigiles_du_roi_Charles_VII_10.jpg/435px-Vigiles_du_roi_Charles_VII_10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Vigiles_du_roi_Charles_VII_10.jpg/580px-Vigiles_du_roi_Charles_VII_10.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vigiles_du_roi_Charles_VII_10.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Miniature of Joan's Execution from <i>The Vigils of King Charles VII</i>, anonymous (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1484, <a href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale_de_France" title="Biblioth√®que nationale de France">Biblioth√®que nationale de France</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>On 28 May, Cauchon went to Joan's cell, along with several other clerics. According to the trial record, Joan said that she had gone back to wearing men's clothes because it was more fitting that she dress like a man while being held with male guards, and that the judges had broken their promise to let her go to mass and to release her from her chains. She stated that if they fulfilled their promises and placed her in a decent prison, she would be obedient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBullough1974[httpswwwjstororgstable2777140seq9_1389]Crane1996[httpswebarchiveorgweb20160705094811httpsfacultysmuedubwheelerjoan_of_arcolr08_clothingjoanofarc_cranepdf_302]Hobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage24_24]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage132_132‚Äì133]Sullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage132_132‚Äì133]_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBullough1974[httpswwwjstororgstable2777140seq9_1389]Crane1996[httpswebarchiveorgweb20160705094811httpsfacultysmuedubwheelerjoan_of_arcolr08_clothingjoanofarc_cranepdf_302]Hobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage24_24]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage132_132‚Äì133]Sullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage132_132‚Äì133]-257">[252]</a></sup> When Cauchon asked about her visions, Joan stated that the voices had blamed her for abjuring out of fear, and that she would not deny them again. As Joan's abjuration had required her to deny her visions, this was sufficient to convict her of relapsing into heresy and to condemn her to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage217_217]Hobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage24_24‚Äì25]_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage217_217]Hobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage24_24‚Äì25]-258">[253]</a></sup> The next day, forty-two assessors were summoned to decide Joan's fate. Two recommended that she be abandoned to the secular courts immediately; the rest recommended that the abjuration be read to her again and explained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage218_218‚Äì219]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage134_134‚Äì135]_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage218_218‚Äì219]PernoudClin1986[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarcherstor00pernpage134_134‚Äì135]-259">[254]</a></sup> In the end, they voted unanimously that Joan was a relapsed heretic, and should be abandoned to the secular power, the English, for punishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage198_198]Sullivan1999Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage139_139]_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobbins2005[httpsarchiveorgdetailstrialofjoanofarc00danipage198_198]Sullivan1999Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage139_139]-260">[255]</a></sup>
</p><p>At about the age of nineteen, Joan was executed on 30 May 1431. In the morning, she was allowed to receive the <a href="/wiki/Sacraments_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Sacraments of the Catholic Church">sacraments</a> despite court process requiring they be denied to heretics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage219_219‚Äì220]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage296_296]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage279_279‚Äì280]_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGies1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclegendr0000giespage219_219‚Äì220]Harrison2014[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclifetra0000harrpage296_296]Lucie-Smith1976[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarc0000lucipage279_279‚Äì280]-261">[256]</a></sup> She was then taken to Rouen's Vieux-March√© (Old Marketplace), where she was publicly read her sentence of condemnation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage148_148]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage225_225]_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESullivan1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsinterrogationofj00sullpage148_148]Taylor2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailsjoanofarclapucel00unsepage225_225]-262">[257]</a></sup> At this point,
